<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Branch of Buddhism</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Maitreya_Detail.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="630" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Maitreya_Detail.jpeg/260px-Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Maitreya_Detail.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Maitreya_Detail.jpeg/390px-Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Maitreya_Detail.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Maitreya_Detail.jpeg/520px-Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Maitreya_Detail.jpeg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Maitreya_Detail.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration in a manuscript of the <i><a href="/wiki/A%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%ADas%C4%81hasrik%C4%81_Praj%C3%B1%C4%81p%C4%81ramit%C4%81_S%C5%ABtra" title="Aṣṭasāhasrikā Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra">Aṣṭasāhasrikā Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nalanda</a>, depicting the bodhisattva <a href="/wiki/Maitreya" title="Maitreya">Maitreya</a>, an important figure in Mahāyāna</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shishoin_temple_shibamata_Five_Tathagatas_2020.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Shishoin_temple_shibamata_Five_Tathagatas_2020.jpg/260px-Shishoin_temple_shibamata_Five_Tathagatas_2020.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Shishoin_temple_shibamata_Five_Tathagatas_2020.jpg/390px-Shishoin_temple_shibamata_Five_Tathagatas_2020.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Shishoin_temple_shibamata_Five_Tathagatas_2020.jpg/520px-Shishoin_temple_shibamata_Five_Tathagatas_2020.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shishoin_temple_shibamata_Five_Tathagatas_2020.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Five_Tath%C4%81gatas" title="Five Tathāgatas">Five Tathāgatas</a> in Shishoin Temple (Tokyo). A unique feature of Mahāyāna is the belief that there are multiple Buddhas which are currently teaching the Dharma</div></div></div>

<li><a href="/wiki/Index_of_Buddhism-related_articles" title="Index of Buddhism-related articles">Index</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_Buddhism" title="Outline of Buddhism">Outline</a></li></ul>
<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P_religion_world.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/P_religion_world.svg/31px-P_religion_world.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/P_religion_world.svg/47px-P_religion_world.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/P_religion_world.svg/62px-P_religion_world.svg.png 2x" width="31"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Religion" title="Portal:Religion">Religion portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Buddhism" title="Template:Buddhism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Buddhism" title="Template talk:Buddhism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Buddhism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><i><b>Mahāyāna</b></i> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>; "Great Vehicle") is a term for a broad group of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> traditions, <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_texts#Mahāyāna_texts" title="Buddhist texts">texts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy" title="Buddhist philosophy">philosophies</a>, and practices. Mahāyāna Buddhism developed in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (c. 1st century BCE onwards) and is considered one of the two main existing branches of Buddhism (the other being <a href="/wiki/Theravada" title="Theravada"><i>Theravāda</i></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:18-1">[1]</a></sup> Mahāyāna accepts the main scriptures and teachings of <a href="/wiki/Early_Buddhism" title="Early Buddhism">early Buddhism</a>, but also recognizes various doctrines and texts which are not accepted by Theravada Buddhism as original. These include the <a href="/wiki/Mahayana_sutras" title="Mahayana sutras">Mahāyāna Sūtras</a> and its emphasis on the <i>bodhisattva</i> path and <a href="/wiki/Prajnaparamita" title="Prajnaparamita"><i>Prajñāpāramitā</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vajray%C4%81na" title="Vajrayāna">Vajrayāna</a></i> or Mantra traditions are a subset of Mahāyāna, which make use of numerous <a href="/wiki/Tantra" title="Tantra">tantric methods</a> considered to be faster and more powerful at achieving <a href="/wiki/Buddhahood" title="Buddhahood">Buddhahood</a> by Vajrayānists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:18-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Mahāyāna" also refers to the path of the <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">bodhisattva</a> striving to become a fully awakened Buddha (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samyaksa%E1%B9%83buddha" title="Samyaksaṃbuddha">samyaksaṃbuddha</a></i>) for the benefit of all sentient beings, and is thus also called the "Bodhisattva Vehicle" (<i>Bodhisattvayāna</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated38_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated38-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[note 1]</a></sup> Mahāyāna Buddhism generally sees the goal of becoming a Buddha through the bodhisattva path as being available to all and sees the state of the <a href="/wiki/Arhat" title="Arhat">arhat</a> as incomplete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> Mahāyāna also includes numerous Buddhas and bodhisattvas that are not found in Theravada (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Amit%C4%81bha" title="Amitābha">Amitābha</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Vairocana" title="Vairocana">Vairocana</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:72_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:72-6">[5]</a></sup> Mahāyāna Buddhist philosophy also promotes unique theories, such as the <a href="/wiki/Madhyamaka" title="Madhyamaka">Madhyamaka</a> theory of emptiness (<i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C5%ABnyat%C4%81" title="Śūnyatā">śūnyatā</a></i>), the <i><a href="/wiki/Yogachara" title="Yogachara">Vijñānavāda</a></i> doctrine and the <i><a href="/wiki/Buddha-nature" title="Buddha-nature">Buddha-nature</a></i> teaching.
</p><p>Although it was initially a small movement in India, Mahāyāna eventually grew to become an influential force in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Buddhism_in_India" title="History of Buddhism in India">Indian Buddhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhead_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhead-7">[6]</a></sup> Large scholastic centers associated with Mahāyāna such as <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nalanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vikramashila" title="Vikramashila">Vikramashila</a> thrived between the seventh and twelfth centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhead_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhead-7">[6]</a></sup> In the course of its history, Mahāyāna Buddhism spread throughout <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>. It remains influential today in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mahāyāna tradition is the largest major tradition of Buddhism existing today (with 53% of Buddhists belonging to <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_Buddhism" title="East Asian Buddhism">East Asian Mahāyāna</a> and 6% to Vajrayāna), compared to 36% for Theravada (survey from 2010).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mahayana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_Sanskrit">Original Sanskrit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mahayana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Original Sanskrit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BuddhistTriad.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="1587" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BuddhistTriad.JPG/220px-BuddhistTriad.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BuddhistTriad.JPG/330px-BuddhistTriad.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BuddhistTriad.JPG/440px-BuddhistTriad.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BuddhistTriad.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mahāyāna Buddhist triad, including <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">Bodhisattva</a> <a href="/wiki/Maitreya" title="Maitreya">Maitreya</a>, the Buddha, and Bodhisattva <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avalokitesvara" title="Avalokitesvara">Avalokiteśvara</a>. 2nd–3rd century CE, <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhāra</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Jan_Nattier" title="Jan Nattier">Jan Nattier</a>, the term <i>Mahāyāna</i> ("Great Vehicle") was originally an honorary synonym for <i>Bodhisattvayāna</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">Bodhisattva</a> Vehicle"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._174_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._174-10">[9]</a></sup> the vehicle of a bodhisattva seeking <a href="/wiki/Buddhahood" title="Buddhahood">buddhahood</a> for the benefit of all sentient beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated38_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated38-3">[3]</a></sup> The term <i>Mahāyāna</i> (which had earlier been used simply as an epithet for Buddhism itself) was therefore adopted at an early date as a synonym for the path and the teachings of the bodhisattvas. Since it was simply an honorary term for <i>Bodhisattvayāna</i>, the adoption of the term <i>Mahāyāna</i> and its application to Bodhisattvayāna did not represent a significant turning point in the development of a Mahāyāna tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._174_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._174-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest Mahāyāna texts, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Sutra" title="Lotus Sutra">Lotus Sūtra</a></i>, often use the term <i>Mahāyāna</i> as a synonym for <i>Bodhisattvayāna</i>, but the term <i><a href="/wiki/Hinayana" title="Hinayana">Hīnayāna</a></i> is comparatively rare in the earliest sources. The presumed dichotomy between <i>Mahāyāna</i> and <i>Hīnayāna</i> can be deceptive, as the two terms were not actually formed in relation to one another in the same era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the earliest and most important references to <i>Mahāyāna</i> are those that occur in the <i><a href="/wiki/Lotus_Sutra" title="Lotus Sutra">Lotus Sūtra</a></i> (Skt. <i>Saddharma Puṇḍarīka Sūtra</i>) dating between the 1st century BCE and the 1st century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Seishi Karashima has suggested that the term first used in an earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C4%81ndh%C4%81r%C4%AB_language" title="Gāndhārī language">Gandhāri</a> <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a> version of the <i>Lotus Sūtra</i> was not the term <i>mahāyāna</i> but the Prakrit word <i>mahājāna</i> in the sense of <i>mahājñāna</i> (great knowing).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2004_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2004-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> At a later stage when the early Prakrit word was converted into Sanskrit, this <i>mahājāna</i>, being phonetically ambivalent, may have been converted into <i>mahāyāna</i>, possibly because of what may have been a double meaning in the famous <a href="/wiki/Upaya#Parable_of_the_burning_house" title="Upaya">Parable of the Burning House</a>, which talks of three vehicles or carts (Skt: <i>yāna</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[note 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2004_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2004-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_translation">Chinese translation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mahayana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chinese translation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>, Mahāyāna is called <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">大乘</span></span> (<i>dacheng</i>), which is a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> of <i>maha</i> (great <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">大</span></span>) <i>yana</i> (vehicle <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">乘</span></span>). There is also the transliteration <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">摩诃衍那</span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> The term appeared in some of the earliest Mahāyāna texts, including <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ling_of_Han" title="Emperor Ling of Han">Emperor Ling of Han</a>'s translation of the Lotus Sutra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._193-1942_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._193-1942-19">[17]</a></sup> It also appears in the Chinese <a href="/wiki/%C4%80gama_(Buddhism)" title="Āgama (Buddhism)">Āgamas</a>, though scholars like Yin Shun argue that this is a later addition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> Some Chinese scholars also argue that the meaning of the term in these earlier texts is different than later ideas of Mahāyāna Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mahayana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seated_Avalokiteshvara._Gandharan,_from_Loriyan_Tangai._Kushan_period,_1st_-_3d_century_AD._Indian_Museum,_Calcutta_ei05-31.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Seated_Avalokiteshvara._Gandharan%2C_from_Loriyan_Tangai._Kushan_period%2C_1st_-_3d_century_AD._Indian_Museum%2C_Calcutta_ei05-31.jpg/220px-Seated_Avalokiteshvara._Gandharan%2C_from_Loriyan_Tangai._Kushan_period%2C_1st_-_3d_century_AD._Indian_Museum%2C_Calcutta_ei05-31.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Seated_Avalokiteshvara._Gandharan%2C_from_Loriyan_Tangai._Kushan_period%2C_1st_-_3d_century_AD._Indian_Museum%2C_Calcutta_ei05-31.jpg/330px-Seated_Avalokiteshvara._Gandharan%2C_from_Loriyan_Tangai._Kushan_period%2C_1st_-_3d_century_AD._Indian_Museum%2C_Calcutta_ei05-31.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Seated_Avalokiteshvara._Gandharan%2C_from_Loriyan_Tangai._Kushan_period%2C_1st_-_3d_century_AD._Indian_Museum%2C_Calcutta_ei05-31.jpg/440px-Seated_Avalokiteshvara._Gandharan%2C_from_Loriyan_Tangai._Kushan_period%2C_1st_-_3d_century_AD._Indian_Museum%2C_Calcutta_ei05-31.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seated_Avalokiteshvara._Gandharan,_from_Loriyan_Tangai._Kushan_period,_1st_-_3d_century_AD._Indian_Museum,_Calcutta_ei05-31.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avalokiteshvara" title="Avalokiteshvara">Avalokiteshvara</a> bodhisattva. <a href="/wiki/Gandharan_Buddhism" title="Gandharan Buddhism">Gandharan</a>, from Loriyan Tangai. <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan</a> period, 1st – 3rd century CE. Indian Museum, <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Calcutta</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karla_chaitya_stupa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Karla_chaitya_stupa.JPG/220px-Karla_chaitya_stupa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Karla_chaitya_stupa.JPG/330px-Karla_chaitya_stupa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Karla_chaitya_stupa.JPG/440px-Karla_chaitya_stupa.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karla_chaitya_stupa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cave complex associated with the <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81s%C4%81%E1%B9%83ghika" title="Mahāsāṃghika">Mahāsāṃghika</a> sect. <a href="/wiki/Karla_Caves" title="Karla Caves">Karla Caves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Mahārāṣtra</a>, India</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mahayana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The origins of Mahāyāna are still not completely understood and there are numerous competing theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirakawa_Akira_1993,_p._260_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirakawa_Akira_1993,_p._260-24">[22]</a></sup> The earliest Western views of Mahāyāna assumed that it existed as a separate school in competition with the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Hinayana" title="Hinayana">Hīnayāna</a>" schools. Some of the major theories about the origins of Mahāyāna include the following:
</p><p><b>The lay origins theory</b> was first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Przyluski" title="Jean Przyluski">Jean Przyluski</a> and then defended by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Lamotte" title="Étienne Lamotte">Étienne Lamotte</a> and Akira Hirakawa. This view states that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Up%C4%81saka_and_Up%C4%81sik%C4%81" title="Upāsaka and Upāsikā">laypersons</a> were particularly important in the development of Mahāyāna and is partly based on some texts like the <a href="/wiki/Vimalakirti_Sutra" title="Vimalakirti Sutra"><i>Vimalakirti Sūtra</i></a>, which praise lay figures at the expense of monastics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirakawa1990271_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirakawa1990271-25">[23]</a></sup> This theory is no longer widely accepted since numerous early Mahāyāna works promote monasticism and asceticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>The Mahāsāṃghika origin theory</b>, which argues that Mahāyāna developed within the <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81s%C4%81%E1%B9%83ghika" title="Mahāsāṃghika">Mahāsāṃghika</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-:12-26">[24]</a></sup> This is defended by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Johan_Hendrik_Caspar_Kern" title="Johan Hendrik Caspar Kern">Hendrik Kern</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.K._Warder" title="A.K. Warder">A.K. Warder</a> and Paul Williams who argue that at least some Mahāyāna elements developed among Mahāsāṃghika communities (from the 1st century BCE onwards), possibly in the area along the <a href="/wiki/Krishna_River" title="Krishna River">Kṛṣṇa River</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh#Early_history" title="Andhra Pradesh">Āndhra</a> region of southern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xing65-66_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xing65-66-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-:22-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirakawa_Akira_1993,_p._263_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirakawa_Akira_1993,_p._263-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-31">[29]</a></sup> The Mahāsāṃghika doctrine of the supramundane (<a href="/wiki/Lokottarav%C4%81da" title="Lokottaravāda"><i>lokottara</i></a>) nature of the Buddha is sometimes seen as a precursor to Mahāyāna views of the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:72_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:72-6">[5]</a></sup> Some scholars also see Mahāyāna figures like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C4%81g%C4%81rjuna" title="Nāgārjuna">Nāgārjuna</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dignaga" title="Dignaga">Dignaga</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Candrak%C4%ABrti" title="Candrakīrti">Candrakīrti</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C4%80ryadeva" title="Āryadeva">Āryadeva</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhavaviveka" title="Bhavaviveka">Bhavaviveka</a> as having ties to the Mahāsāṃghika tradition of Āndhra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> However, other scholars have also pointed to different regions as being important, such as <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> and northwest India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[note 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mahāsāṃghika origins theory has also slowly been shown to be problematic by scholarship that revealed how certain Mahāyāna sutras show traces of having developed among other <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikaya_School" title="Nikaya School">nikāyas</a></i> or monastic orders (such as the <a href="/wiki/Dharmaguptaka" title="Dharmaguptaka">Dharmaguptaka</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> Because of such evidence, scholars like Paul Harrison and Paul Williams argue that the movement was not sectarian and was possibly pan-buddhist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-:12-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup> There is no evidence that Mahāyāna ever referred to a separate formal school or sect of Buddhism, but rather that it existed as a certain set of ideals, and later doctrines, for aspiring bodhisattvas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._193-1942_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._193-1942-19">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>The "forest hypothesis"</b> meanwhile states that Mahāyāna arose mainly among "hard-core <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">ascetics</a>, members of the forest dwelling (<i>aranyavasin</i>) wing of the Buddhist Order", who were attempting to imitate the Buddha's forest living.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> This has been defended by Paul Harrison, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Nattier" title="Jan Nattier">Jan Nattier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Ray" title="Reginald Ray">Reginald Ray</a>. This theory is based on certain sutras like the <i><a href="/wiki/Ugraparip%E1%B9%9Bcch%C4%81_S%C5%ABtra" title="Ugraparipṛcchā Sūtra">Ugraparipṛcchā Sūtra</a></i> and the <i>Mahāyāna Rāṣṭrapālapaṛiprcchā</i> which promote ascetic practice in the wilderness as a superior and elite path. These texts criticize monks who live in cities and denigrate the forest life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENattier2003193–4_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENattier2003193–4-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jan Nattier's study of the <i>Ugraparipṛcchā Sūtra, A few good men</i> (2003) argues that this sutra represents the earliest form of Mahāyāna, which presents the <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">bodhisattva</a> path as a 'supremely difﬁcult enterprise' of elite monastic forest asceticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-:12-26">[24]</a></sup> Boucher's study on the <i>Rāṣṭrapālaparipṛcchā-sūtra</i> (2008) is another recent work on this subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>The cult of the book theory</b>, defended by <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Schopen" title="Gregory Schopen">Gregory Schopen</a>, states that Mahāyāna arose among a number of loosely connected book worshiping groups of monastics, who studied, memorized, copied and revered particular Mahāyāna sūtras. Schopen thinks they were inspired by cult shrines where Mahāyāna sutras were kept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-:12-26">[24]</a></sup> Schopen also argued that these groups mostly rejected <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupa</a> worship, or worshiping holy relics.
</p><p>David Drewes has recently argued against all of the major theories outlined above. He points out that there is no actual evidence for the existence of book shrines, that the practice of sutra veneration was pan-Buddhist and not distinctly Mahāyāna. Furthermore, Drewes argues that "Mahāyāna sutras advocate mnemic/oral/aural practices more frequently than they do written ones."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-:12-26">[24]</a></sup> Regarding the forest hypothesis, he points out that only a few Mahāyāna sutras directly advocate forest dwelling, while the others either do not mention it or see it as unhelpful, promoting easier practices such as "merely listening to the sutra, or thinking of particular Buddhas, that they claim can enable one to be reborn in special, luxurious '<a href="/wiki/Pure_Land_Buddhism" title="Pure Land Buddhism">pure lands</a>' where one will be able to make easy and rapid progress on the bodhisattva path and attain Buddhahood after as little as one lifetime."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-:12-26">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Drewes states that the evidence merely shows that "Mahāyāna was primarily a textual movement, focused on the revelation, preaching, and dissemination of <a href="/wiki/Mahayana_sutras" title="Mahayana sutras">Mahāyāna sutras</a>, that developed within, and never really departed from, traditional Buddhist social and institutional structures."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-42">[39]</a></sup> Drewes points out the importance of <i>dharmabhanakas</i> (preachers, reciters of these sutras) in the early Mahāyāna sutras. This figure is widely praised as someone who should be respected, obeyed ('as a slave serves his lord'), and donated to, and it is thus possible these people were the primary agents of the Mahāyāna movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Early_Mah.C4.81y.C4.81na"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Mahāyāna">Early Mahāyāna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mahayana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early Mahāyāna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest textual evidence of "Mahāyāna" comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C5%ABtras" title="Sūtras">sūtras</a> ("discourses", scriptures) originating around the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">the common era</a>. Jan Nattier has noted that some of the earliest Mahāyāna texts, such as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ugrapariprccha_Sutra" title="Ugrapariprccha Sutra">Ugraparipṛccha Sūtra</a></i> use the term "Mahāyāna", yet there is no doctrinal difference between Mahāyāna in this context and the <a href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_schools" title="Early Buddhist schools">early schools</a>. Instead, Nattier writes that in the earliest sources, "Mahāyāna" referred to the rigorous emulation of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>'s path to Buddhahood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._193-1942_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nattier,_Jan_2003_p._193-1942-19">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some important evidence for early Mahāyāna Buddhism comes from the texts translated by the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indoscythian</a> monk <a href="/wiki/Lokaksema_(Buddhist_monk)" title="Lokaksema (Buddhist monk)">Lokakṣema</a> in the 2nd century CE, who came to China from the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhāra</a>. These are some of the earliest known Mahāyāna texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[note 4]</a></sup> Study of these texts by Paul Harrison and others show that they strongly promote <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monasticism</a> (contra the lay origin theory), acknowledge the legitimacy of <a href="/wiki/Arhat" title="Arhat">arhatship</a>, do not recommend devotion towards 'celestial' <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">bodhisattvas</a> and do not show any attempt to establish a new sect or order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_26-7"><a href="#cite_note-:12-26">[24]</a></sup> A few of these texts often emphasize <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">ascetic</a> practices, forest dwelling, and deep states of meditative concentration (<i><a href="/wiki/Samadhi" title="Samadhi">samadhi</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Indian Mahāyāna never had nor ever attempted to have a separate <a href="/wiki/Vinaya" title="Vinaya">Vinaya</a> or ordination lineage from the early schools of Buddhism, and therefore each <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu" title="Bhikkhu">bhikṣu</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhikkhuni" title="Bhikkhuni">bhikṣuṇī</a> adhering to the Mahāyāna formally belonged to one of the early Buddhist schools. Membership in these <i>nikāyas</i>, or monastic orders, continues today, with the <a href="/wiki/Dharmaguptaka" title="Dharmaguptaka">Dharmaguptaka</a> nikāya being used in East Asia, and the <a href="/wiki/Mulasarvastivada" title="Mulasarvastivada">Mūlasarvāstivāda</a> nikāya being used in <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhism</a>. Therefore, Mahāyāna was never a separate monastic sect outside of the early schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul Harrison clarifies that while monastic Mahāyānists belonged to a nikāya, not all members of a nikāya were Mahāyānists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup> From Chinese monks visiting India, we now know that both Mahāyāna and non-Mahāyāna monks in India often lived in the same monasteries side by side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[45]</a></sup> It is also possible that, formally, Mahāyāna would have been understood as a group of monks or nuns within a larger monastery taking a vow together (known as a "<i>kriyākarma</i>") to memorize and study a Mahāyāna text or texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup>
</p>

ashoka sya.svg" data-file-height="64" data-file-width="64" decoding="async" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gupta_ashoka_sya.svg/25px-Gupta_ashoka_sya.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gupta_ashoka_sya.svg/50px-Gupta_ashoka_sya.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a></sub> <sub><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gupta_ashoka_a.svg"><img
